        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.8b15274"><meta property="og:title" content="Marriage of an Ether-Based Electrolyte with Hard Carbon Anodes Creates Superior Sodium-Ion Batteries with High Mass Loading"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/acsami.8b15274.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Inferior rate performance, insufficient cycle life, and low mass loading have restricted the practical application of hard carbon (HC) anodes in sodium-ion batteries (NIBs). Here, a compatible strategy is developed by matching HC anodes with an ether-based electrolyte. Systematical investigation reveals that good compatibility of the electrode–electrolyte systems forms thinner but a more sustainable solid-electrolyte interphase and delivers a higher ionic conductivity and Na+ ion diffusion coefficient than the commonly used ester-based electrolytes. Therefore, an excellent electrochemical performance is demonstrated with a long cycle life (∼196 mA h/g and 90% capacity retention after 2000 cycles at 1 A/g), a super rate capability (∼51% capacity retention at 10 A/g) at a mass loading of 1.5 mg/cm2, and a high initial Coulombic efficiency of 85.9%. More importantly, a high reversible areal capacity of 4.3 mA h/cm2 can be achieved at an ultrahigh mass loading of 17 mg/cm2, superior to all reported HC anodes. Our findings not only shed light on the design of high-performance battery systems but also promise a commercial transformation from the lab test to mass production of NIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/acsami.8b15274.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da90ac492db325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da90ac492db325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '7fee3adf-411e-4332-b48a-3e94757cdd52'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da90ac492db325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Marriage of an Ether-Based Electrolyte with Hard Carbon Anodes Creates Superior Sodium-Ion Batteries with High Mass Loading", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b15274", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Marriage of an Ether-Based Electrolyte with Hard Carbon Anodes Creates Superior Sodium-Ion Batteries with High Mass Loading", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b15274", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Marriage of an Ether-Based Electrolyte with Hard Carbon Anodes Creates Superior Sodium-Ion Batteries with High Mass Loading", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b15274", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Marriage of an Ether-Based Electrolyte with Hard Carbon Anodes Creates Superior Sodium-Ion Batteries with High Mass Loading", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b15274", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Marriage of an Ether-Based Electrolyte with Hard Carbon Anodes Creates Superior Sodium-Ion Batteries with High Mass Loading", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b15274", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Marriage of an Ether-Based Electrolyte with Hard Carbon Anodes Creates Superior Sodium-Ion Batteries with High Mass Loading", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b15274", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Marriage of an Ether-Based Electrolyte with Hard Carbon Anodes Creates Superior Sodium-Ion Batteries with High Mass Loading", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b15274", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Marriage of an Ether-Based Electrolyte with Hard Carbon Anodes Creates Superior Sodium-Ion Batteries with High Mass Loading", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b15274", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Marriage of an Ether-Based Electrolyte with Hard Carbon Anodes Creates Superior Sodium-Ion Batteries with High Mass Loading", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b15274", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

LONDON"></a></div></div></div><div class="article_header-meta"><div class="article_header-meta-left"><input type="hidden" name="journalCodeForjhpLink" value="aamick"><input type="hidden" class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D41380%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYongwu%2BHe%252C%2BPanxing%2BBai%252C%2BShuyan%2BGao%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D48%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b15274%26title%3DMarriage%2Bof%2Ban%2BEther-Based%2BElectrolyte%2Bwith%2BHard%2BCarbon%2BAnodes%2BCreates%2BSuperior%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%2Bwith%2BHigh%2BMass%2BLoading%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D41388%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2018" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/medium/am-2018-15274t_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/large/am-2018-15274t_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b15274&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Inferior rate performance, insufficient cycle life, and low mass loading have restricted the practical application of hard carbon (HC) anodes in sodium-ion batteries (NIBs). Here, a compatible strategy is developed by matching HC anodes with an ether-based electrolyte. Systematical investigation reveals that good compatibility of the electrode–electrolyte systems forms thinner but a more sustainable solid-electrolyte interphase and delivers a higher ionic conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion coefficient than the commonly used ester-based electrolytes. Therefore, an excellent electrochemical performance is demonstrated with a long cycle life (∼196 mA h/g and 90% capacity retention after 2000 cycles at 1 A/g), a super rate capability (∼51% capacity retention at 10 A/g) at a mass loading of 1.5 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>, and a high initial Coulombic efficiency of 85.9%. More importantly, a high reversible areal capacity of 4.3 mA h/cm<sup>2</sup> can be achieved at an ultrahigh mass loading of 17 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>, superior to all reported HC anodes. Our findings not only shed light on the design of high-performance battery systems but also promise a commercial transformation from the lab test to mass production of NIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D41380%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYongwu%2BHe%252C%2BPanxing%2BBai%252C%2BShuyan%2BGao%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D48%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b15274%26title%3DMarriage%2Bof%2Ban%2BEther-Based%2BElectrolyte%2Bwith%2BHard%2BCarbon%2BAnodes%2BCreates%2BSuperior%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%2Bwith%2BHigh%2BMass%2BLoading%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D41388%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292120&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrolytes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrolytes" class="article__tags__link">Electrolytes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Ions" class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff"                     </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Hard+Carbon+Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_hard%20carbon%20anode">hard carbon anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ether-based+Electrolyte&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ether-based%20electrolyte">ether-based electrolyte</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_interfacial%20kinetics">interfacial kinetics</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High+Mass-loading+Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high%20mass-loading%20anode">high mass-loading anode</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), as the most successful energy storage system, have been widely deployed in portable electronic devices and electric vehicles because of their long cycle life and high energy density. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> With the growing demand for LIBs, the limited reserves and uneven geographical distribution of lithium in the Earth’s crust will certainly trigger resources and cost issues and therein impede their large-scale applications in the future. With the advantages of abundance and facile accessibility of sodium resources and the similar intercalation chemistry between sodium and lithium, sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) have recently attracted increasing attention as a promising candidate for large-scale stationary energy storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 2 and 3">(2,3)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">In contrast to the considerable progress made for cathode materials in NIBs, the exploration of anode materials remains a great challenge. Graphite, the standard anode material in commercial LIBs, does not function well for NIBs due to the larger radius of Na<sup>+</sup> ions (1.02 Å) compared to that of Li<sup>+</sup> ions (0.76 Å) and thermodynamic instability of sodium–graphite intercalation compounds, giving a low specific capacity either in ester-based electrolytes or in ether-based electrolytes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4,5)</a> Unremitting efforts have been devoted to prescreening anode materials for NIBs, such as carbonaceous materials (mainly amorphous carbons), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 6 to 11">(6−11)</a> alloy-based materials (Sb, P, Sn, and Bi), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 12 to 18">(12−18)</a> oxides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19,20)</a> sulfides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 21 to 23">(21−23)</a> and organic compounds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> Carbonaceous materials hold good resiliency and conductivity, which provide a robust mechanical support to alleviate the volume changes caused by Na ion insertion and ensures a good electronic conductivity, making them the ideal host materials to NIBs. Among them, hard carbon (HC) anodes show the most promising performance with a capacity of 250–400 mA h/g and a low voltage plateau of ∼0.1 V. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 4, 26 and 27">(4,26,27)</a> Particularly, HC materials can be prepared from abundant and renewable biomass resources, which meets the low-cost requirement for mass manufacture. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 28 to 37">(28−37)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">However, the HC anodes are often subjected to a poor rate performance where most failed at a current density of 1 A/g or lower and insufficient lifespan, making them less competitive for practical applications. Recently, the tailored electrolytes and solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) layers have been employed to improve the performance of carbon anode materials in NIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 38 to 40">(38−40)</a> Yang’ group reported that the optimized SEI layers formed in an ether-based electrolyte can be conducive to achieve superb sodium storage performance for reduced graphene oxide anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Similarly, Zhou’s group employed an ether-based electrolyte to reduce the electrochemical polarization and modify the SEI layers, which enhanced the rate capability of HC anodes, but still a poor cycling stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Very recently, we reported a new strategy to stabilize the HC anodes in an ether-based electrolyte by preengineering a “foreign SEI” from an ester-based electrolyte and demonstrated a significantly improved electrochemical performance with no detectable capacity loss over 1000 cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> However, sustained efforts are still needed to produce better HC anodes that simultaneously possess high rate capability and long-term cycling stability.</div><div class="NLM_p">Besides the good electrochemical performance of the HC anodes, high mass loading is another critical parameter for practical applications. So far, the mass loading of HC anodes reported in the literature is too low, mostly around 1.0 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>, delivering a specific areal capacity of 0.3 mA h/cm<sup>2</sup> (given the gravimetric specific capacity of the HC anodes is 300 mA h/g). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref35 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref35 ref41" aria-label="reference 27, 35 and 41">(27,35,41)</a> Recently, improved mass loadings have been achieved in some carbon anodes for NIBs, reaching 4 mg/cm<sup>2</sup> with an areal specific capacity of 1.2 mA h/cm<sup>2</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref42" aria-label="reference 28 and 42">(28,42)</a> However, there still exists a big gap between the lab test results and the commercial requirement (∼3.7 mA h/cm<sup>2</sup> for graphite anodes in commercial LIBs). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, an ether-based electrolyte was applied to circumvent the challenges that the HC anodes are facing in NIBs. The ether-based electrolyte performs a better wettability, creates a thinner but a more sustainable SEI, and delivers a higher ionic conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion coefficient in HC compared with the widely used ester-based electrolytes and thus enabling an excellent electrochemical performance of HC anodes in terms of high initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 85.9%, long cycle life (retaining 196 mA h/g after 2000 cycles at 1 A/g), high rate capability (delivering 139 mA h/g at a very high rate of 10 A/g), and above all, high mass loading (reaching 17 mg/cm<sup>2</sup> with a high specific areal capacity of 4.3 mA h/cm<sup>2</sup>). The specific areal capacity is not only much better than those HC anodes reported in the literature but also comparable with those commercial graphite anodes in LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref35 ref41 ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref35 ref41 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 27, 35, 41, 43 and 44">(27,35,41,43,44)</a> The compatibility between the ether-based electrolyte and HC anodes contributes to the superior kinetics and high energy density and makes it possible for practical applications.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Synthesis of HC</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Chitosan (degree of deacetylation ≥95%, viscosity 100–200 MPa·s, Aladdin Co.,) was used without further purification for preparation of HC by pyrolysis. It was carbonized at 1100 °C for 2 h in the N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere using a quartz glass tube furnace. The yield is 30%.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology of produced materials was investigated using Hitachi S-4800 (Japan) scanning electron microscopy (SEM). Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) was performed on JEOL-JEM-2100F (Japan). X-ray diffraction (XRD) was conducted on Bruker-D8 ADVANCE (Germany) with a Cu Kα radiation source (<i>V</i> = 40 kV, <i>I</i> = 40 mA, and λ = 1.5418 Å). Raman spectroscopy was carried out on a Thermo Fisher Scientific-DXR (USA) with a 532 nm laser. CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms were measured on a Micromeritics ASAP 2420-4 volumetric adsorption analyzer (USA). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was carried out on EDAX-GENESIS (USA) with a mono Al Kα radiation.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrochemical Measurement</h3><div class="NLM_p">The HC anodes were prepared by casting the slurry consisting of 90 wt % HC powder and 10 wt % sodium alginate binder (in a 10 mg/mL aqueous solution) on Cu foils and dried at 110 °C in a vacuum oven overnight. The HC anodes with different mass loadings of 1.5, 5.0, 7.0, 12.0, and 17.0 mg/cm<sup>2</sup> were prepared. CR2032 type coin cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox (Mikrouna, universal 2440, China) with H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> &lt; 0.1 ppm. Sodium metal (Na, Sigma-Aldrich, USA) was used as counter and reference electrodes. The ether-based electrolyte is 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in ethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DME), and the ester-based electrolyte is 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (1:1, v/v). The ionic conductibility was measured at 25 °C using an ionic conductivity meter DDSJ-308A (Shanghai Hongyi Instrument Co. Ltd.).</div><div class="NLM_p last">The galvanostatic discharge/charge cycling performance was tested on LAND CT2001A battery testers at a voltage range of 0–2.5 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were carried out on a CHI 600 electrochemical test station at a scan rate of 0.1 mV/s between 0 and 2.5 V. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were conducted on a frequency response analyzer (Solartron, SI 1260) in the frequency range from 0.01 to 10<sup>6</sup> Hz with an AC signal amplitude of 5 mV.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">HC was synthesized by directly pyrolyzing chitosan in the N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). The morphology of the HC was examined by SEM and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, the HC has a random granular shape with a particle size of &lt;10 μm. The HRTEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b) indicates an amorphous structure, a typical feature of HC, consisting of numerous randomly oriented and locally ordered nanocrystallites and micropores. The interlayer spacing within the nanocrystallites is measured to be 0.363 nm, larger than that of graphite (0.335 nm). The expanded interlayer spacing should be induced by the incorporation of heteroatoms of oxygen and nitrogen and/or defective carbon structures. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45 ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 45 to 47">(45−47)</a> The randomly oriented structure was further confirmed by the XRD pattern shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c, where only broad peaks at 2θ = 24.4° and 43.3° are presented [corresponding to (002) and (101) planes, respectively], indicative of an amorphous structure. The average interlayer distance was calculated to be 0.366 nm, consistent with the observation by HRTEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). The Raman spectrum of the HC also reveals the amorphous and crystallite structures by two broad peaks at 1361 and 1595 cm<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d), which corresponds to the D-band of defects and the G-band of graphitic carbon, respectively. This is in good agreement with the HRTEM and XRD measurement results. The porous structure of the HC was investigated by CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption measurement (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). A low specific surface area of 47.6 m<sup>2</sup>/g was measured, which is in favor of reducing the formation of SEI layers and thus improves the ICE of HC anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 48 and 49">(48,49)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/medium/am-2018-15274t_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/large/am-2018-15274t_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Structure characterization: (a) SEM and (b) HRTEM images, (c) XRD pattern, (d) Raman spectrum and high-resolution XPS spectra of (e) C 1s, (f) O 1s, and (g) N 1s of the HC.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/large/am-2018-15274t_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b15274&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">XPS was performed to analyze the element composition and the chemical states of the HC. C 1s, N 1s, and O 1s peaks were detected at 284.6, 401.4, and 531.4 eV in the XPS survey spectrum with the atomic contents of 93.7, 1.4, and 4.9%, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). The high-resolution spectrum of the C 1s peak can be fitted into four peaks of sp<sup>2</sup> C (284.5 eV), −CH<sub>2</sub>– (284.9 eV), C–O (286.0 eV), C═O (287.3 eV), and O═C–O (289.2 eV) bonds (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> The high-resolution O 1s core-level spectrum was deconvoluted into two peaks at 531.3 and 532.6 eV (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f), corresponding to C═O and C–O bonds, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> The N consists of three different circumstances: pyridinic N (N-6) at 398.4 eV, pyrrolic N (N-5) at 400.9 eV, and quaternary N (N-Q) at 401.4 eV (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>g). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 50 to 52">(50−52)</a> The quaternary N can increase the conductivity of carbon materials as a result of the introduction of extra free electrons, whereas N-5 and N-6 can adsorb exotic atoms, thus functioning as Na storage sites. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 52 and 53">(52,53)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">In order to explore the electrochemical performance of the HC, it was evaluated in half coin cells using an ether-based electrolyte of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in ethylene glycol DME with a mass loading of 1.5 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a shows the galvanostatic discharge/charge (sodiation/desodiation) profiles of the HC anodes in the initial three cycles at a current density of 0.02 A/g and a voltage window of 0–2.5 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. A high-voltage slope (&gt;0.1 V) followed by a plateau below 0.1 V was displayed, which is a characteristic behavior of HC anode materials in NIBs. The high-voltage sloping region is attributed to sodium adsorption on the defect sites or heteroatoms, whereas the low-voltage plateau corresponds to sodium filling into the micropores. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54 ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 54 and 55">(54,55)</a> Micropores with pore sizes of &lt;0.8 nm were clearly measured (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>). According to our previous work, where the pore-filling mechanism at the low-voltage plateau was clearly verified, the plateau capacity in this work is attributed to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> in the micropores. A high reversible capacity of 275.4 mA h/g was delivered with an ICE of 85.9%, which is among the best HC anodes reported in the literature (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). Besides the extremely low specific surface area of the HC material (47.6 m<sup>2</sup>/g), the utilization of the ether-based electrolyte was in favor of increasing the ICEs of the HC anodes in NIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 48 and 49">(48,49)</a> The ICE of the HC anodes is 75.0% in the ester-based electrolyte, much lower than that in the ether-based electrolyte. The enhanced ICE in the ether-based electrolyte should be resulted from the less decomposition of the electrolyte to form thinner SEI films on the HC surface compared with that in the ester-based electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 38 and 39">(38,39)</a> The CV curves were collected at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV/s and in the voltage window of 0–2.5 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). A strong reversible redox couple at 0.06/0.10 V should result from the pore filling process, and a small irreversible peak at 0.59 V in the first cathodic process was assigned to the formation of SEI layers on the electrode surface and disappeared in the following cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> The overlapped desodiation curves in both galvanostatic discharge/charge and CV measurements strongly suggest a high reversibility of HC anodes.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/medium/am-2018-15274t_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/large/am-2018-15274t_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Electrochemical performance: (a) galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles, (b) cyclic voltammogram curves at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV/s, and cycling performance at (c) 0.05, and (d) 2 A/g of the HC anodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/large/am-2018-15274t_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b15274&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The cycling stability of the HC anodes was first examined at a current density of 50 mA/g. After a slight capacity decline in the initial few cycles, no further capacity decay was observed up to 300 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c), demonstrating an exceptional stability. This is further confirmed by 90% capacity retention (196 mA h/g) after 2000 cycles at 1 A/g (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d). The galvanostatic discharge/charge voltage profiles of the HC anodes in selected cycles are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. The performance outperforms all those HC anodes for NIBs ever reported in the literature (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">The rate capability of the HC anodes was evaluated at different discharge/charge current densities from 0.02 to 10 A/g with the ether-based electrolyte. For comparison, the HC electrodes were also tested in the same conditions except for the use of an ester-based electrolyte of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC and DMC (1:1, v/v), a commonly used electrolyte for NIBs in the literature (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a). The corresponding discharge/charge profiles were shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b for the ether-based electrolyte and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c for the ester-based electrolyte. The HC anodes exhibited an extraordinary rate capability with a high capacity retention of 206 mA h/g at a current rate of 2 A/g in the ether-based electrolyte and 139 mA h/g even at a very high current density of 10 A/g (equal to 51% capacity retention of that at 0.02 A/g). Such high capacity retention has never been reported for HC anode materials in NIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). In contrast, with the ester-based electrolyte, the capacity suffers a fast capacity scroll-off as the current density increases and only 60 mA h/g is remained at 2 A/g, which is in agreement with those previous reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref53" aria-label="reference 31 and 53">(31,53)</a> Note that the similar reversible capacities at low current rates suggest that the Na storage in the HC is thermodynamically the same in the two kinds of electrolytes but is kinetically controlled at higher current densities as shown by the huge gap in capacities. As current density increases, the capacity fading mainly comes from the sloping region in the ether-based electrolyte but the plateau region in the ester-based electrolyte (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d,e), which clearly reveals the significant kinetics difference between the two electrolytes.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/medium/am-2018-15274t_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/large/am-2018-15274t_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Comparison of the rate behavior of the HC electrodes in ether- and ester-based electrolytes: (a) rate performance of the HC electrodes at symmetric discharge/charge current rates, the discharge/charge voltage profiles in the symmetric current rates with (b) ether-based electrolyte and (c) ester-based electrolyte, analysis of the sloping and plateau region capacity contribution with (d) ether-based electrolyte and (e) ester-based electrolyte, the discharge/charge voltage profiles at a low discharge current of 0.02 A/g and different charge currents with (f) ether-based electrolyte and (g) ester-based electrolyte, and (h) capacity retention and (i) overpotential at a discharge rate of 0.02 A/g and different charge current rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/large/am-2018-15274t_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b15274&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The rate capability was examined by sodiating at a low rate of 0.02 A/g to make sure a complete sodiation reaction but desodiating at different rates (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f,g). Surprisingly, different from the rate performance obtained with symmetric sodiation/desodiation current densities (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b,c), similar desodiation capacities were delivered in the two kinds of electrolytes in the asymmetric current rate tests (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>h), 215.9 mA h/g for the ether-based electrolyte and 206.2 mA h/g for the ester-based electrolyte at 5 A/g. The difference between the two test protocols is attributed to the large desodiation voltage window (Δ<i>V</i><sub>des</sub>, voltage difference between average desodiation voltage of the plateau at 0.02 A/g and cutoff voltage) and the small sodiation voltage window (Δ<i>V</i><sub>sod</sub>, voltage difference between average sodiation voltage of the plateau at 0.02 A/g and cutoff voltage) as well as the kinetics difference in the two electrolytes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>). Δ<i>V</i><sub>sod</sub> is 0.071 and 0.079 V for the ether-based and ester-based electrolytes, respectively. It can be seen from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>i that the overpotential (η) at high current rates is much larger for the ester-based electrolyte than the ether-based electrolyte (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>). Taking the plateau voltage at 0.02 A/g as a baseline (0.079 and 0.071 V, respectively), η at 5 A/g is 0.654 V for the ester-based electrolyte, whereas it is only 0.088 V for the ether-based electrolyte. If η &lt; Δ<i>V</i><sub>sod</sub>, the HC anodes can be completely sodiated, whereas in the case of η &gt; Δ<i>V</i><sub>sod</sub>, Na ions are difficult to insert into the HC. Assuming that the overpotentials for sodiation and desodiation are same at the same current rates, at 1.0 A/g, η is 0.116 V in the ester-based electrolyte, larger than Δ<sub>sod</sub> (0.091 V), leading to an incomplete Na ion insertion and thereby lower sodiation capacity (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>i). In the ether-based electrolyte, η is small even at 10 A/g (0.167 V), enabling an efficient sodiation reaction and thus a high capacity. During the desodiation processes, Δ<i>V</i><sub>des</sub> is large enough to tolerate even at a high current rate. As a result, a complete desodiation will occur either in ether- or ester-based electrolytes and thus a high capacity retention upon a complete sodiation.</div><div class="NLM_p">The disparate overpotentials in the two electrolytes depend on the significant different reaction kinetics. The reaction kinetics of the HC anodes in the two electrolytes was investigated using EIS to account for the rate of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in electrolytes and passing through the SEI films. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a compares the Nyquist plots of two electrolytes. The impedance is much smaller in the ether-based electrolyte than that in the ester-based electrolyte, indicating a faster kinetics in the former one. The impedance data were fitted with equivalent circuits as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. The resistance of <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> represents the electrolyte resistance and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> correspond to the charge transfer resistance and the contact resistance related to the SEI layers, respectively. The electrolyte resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>) is 4.6 Ω in the ether-based electrolyte and 5.3 Ω in the ester-based electrolyte, showing a higher ion movability in the ether-based electrolyte. This is also consistent with the ionic conductivity test results by a test meter, where the ether-based electrolyte has a Na<sup>+</sup> ionic conductivity of 15.6 mS/cm much larger than that of 8.6 mS/cm of the ester-based electrolyte. The charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) is mainly associated with the electronic conductivity and the ionic diffusion coefficient. Therefore, higher ionic diffusion coefficient of the ether-based electrolyte responds to the smaller <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>. The smaller contact resistance (<i>R</i><sub>f</sub>) in the ether-based electrolyte is verified by the thinner SEI layers formed on the electrode surface, confirmed by SEM images which still clearly show the HC particle shapes without any distinct shroud after 100 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b) compared with the pristine electrolyte (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). Surprisingly, the surface of the electrolyte shows less change of outline of the HC particles even after 2000 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). In contrast, the HC particles are encapsulated by a thick SEI layer and can be difficult to discern in the ester-based electrolyte after 100 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). These demonstrate a thin and sustainable SEI layer in the ether-based electrolyte. The chemical property of the SEI films formed in the two different electrolytes was investigated by XPS (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). The SP<sup>2</sup> C peak is relatively weaker and the O–C═O/C═O and Na–O peaks are stronger in the EC/DMC electrolyte-originated SEI films than in the DME electrolyte-originated SEI films, indicating that a larger amount of decomposition products were produced in the ester-based electrolyte, in accordance with a thicker SEI film. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref40" aria-label="reference 38 and 40">(38,40)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/medium/am-2018-15274t_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/large/am-2018-15274t_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Kinetics characterization of the HC electrode in ether- and ester-based electrolytes. (a) Nyquist plots and fitting curves after several cycles (both at 1.8 V). The hollow circles show the experimental data and lines represent the fitting results, respectively. Morphology observed by SEM in (b) ether- and (c) ester-based electrolyte after 100 cycles. Scale bar, 5 μm. (d) Wettability of ether- and ester-based electrolytes on the HC electrodes. (e) Logarithm diffusion coefficient (log <i>D</i>) at different states of charge in discharge and charge process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/large/am-2018-15274t_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b15274&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The reaction kinetics of HC in different electrolytes also depends on the wettability of the electrolyte with HC electrodes. It has been reported that the interaction between the electrode materials and the electrolytes may produce significant effects on the formation of SEI layers and thus the electrochemical performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56 ref57 ref58 ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56 ref57 ref58 ref59" aria-label="reference 56 to 59">(56−59)</a> In our case, the wettability of HC electrodes was tested with the two kinds of electrolytes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d). The ether-based electrolyte can fully wet the HC electrodes as shown by the rapid spread upon the contact with the electrolytes (contact angles nearly 0°). In contrast, the ester-based electrolyte presents a contact angle of 33.1°, indicating a poor wettability. The better wettability suggests a stronger interaction between the electrolytes and the electrodes, benefiting the formation of a thin and sustainable SEI layer and thus reducing the interfacial resistance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The reaction kinetics of HC in different electrolytes lies on the diffusion kinetics as well. To further evaluate Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion properties in the HC particles with the two electrolytes, the Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>) at different sodiation/desodiation states were calculated using the Warburg diffusion in the low-frequency linear region of EIS. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61 ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 61 and 62">(61,62)</a> The computation is described in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>. <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> in the ether-based electrolyte (∼10<sup>–14</sup>) is found to be an order of magnitude higher than that of the ester-based electrolyte (∼10<sup>–15</sup>) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e), meaning a much faster transfer of Na<sup>+</sup> ions within the HC particles when the ether-based electrolyte is used. The use of the ether-based electrolyte affords a higher ionic conductivity, produces a thinner SEI layer, offers a larger ion diffusion coefficient, and finally results in an outstanding rate capability. The sodiation and desodiation behaviors of the HC anodes in the ether- and ester-based electrolytes are schematically illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/medium/am-2018-15274t_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/large/am-2018-15274t_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Schematic illustration of the kinetics in HC anode for NIBs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/large/am-2018-15274t_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b15274&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To verify the improved sodiation and desodiation kinetics in the ether-based electrolyte, HC electrodes with different mass loadings were fabricated from a common value of 1.5 mg/cm<sup>2</sup> to an ultrahigh value of 17 mg/cm<sup>2</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10a</a>). The excellent electrochemical performance and outstanding rate capability of the HC anode in the ether-based electrolyte with high ionic conductivity and superior wettability make the high mass-loading electrodes possible for practical commercial applications. The thicknesses of the HC anodes were measured to be 14 μm for 1.5 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>, 47 μm for 5.0 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>, 65 μm for 7.0 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>, 117 μm for 12 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>, and 195 μm for 17 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10b,c</a>), indicating a proportional correlation between the thickness and the mass loading. The photographs and SEM images of the electrodes with different mass loadings reveal a similar surface morphology, showing that the mass-loading variation does not inflict obvious influence on the film quality of the HC electrodes. The uniformity of the electrodes with different thicknesses was further confirmed by the cross-sectional SEM images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10d</a>). A compact and uniform distribution of the active material was displayed for all electrodes even for the ultrahigh mass loading of 17 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>. Such high mass loading has never been reported for HC anodes in NIBs and it is comparable with those of commercial graphite anodes in LIBs (15 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>), which promises commercial applications for high energy NIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref43" aria-label="reference 29 and 43">(29,43)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">In order to prove the feasibility of high mass loading in NIBs with HC anodes, the electrochemical performance of the HC anodes with different mass loadings was evaluated by the galvanostatic discharge/charge test at the current density of 0.02 A/g. Typical HC voltage profiles are displayed for all mass-loading anodes with a voltage slope at high voltage (&gt;0.1 V) followed by a voltage plateau below 0.1 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a). Surprisingly, high reversible capacities of &gt;250 mA h/g were delivered for all mass-loading electrodes and a high areal specific capacity of 4.3 mA h/cm<sup>2</sup> can be achieved (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b) with a very high ICE of 85.4% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c), which are among the highest values for the HC anodes in NIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). The thickness and electrochemical performance of the HC anodes with different mass loadings are summarized in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S5</a>. The good electrochemical performance of the HC electrodes with high mass loadings is unquestionably attributed to the enhanced electronic conductivity of the nitrogen-doping HC and the outstanding kinetics boosted by the ether-based electrolyte. The cycling stability of the HC anodes with different mass loadings was tested at 0.2 A/g after two activation cycles at 0.02 A/g (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d). Stable cycling performance was demonstrated for all electrodes with negligible capacity loss over 150 cycles. The surface morphology and structure of the electrodes after the cycling test were examined using photomicroscopy and SEM (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/suppl_file/am8b15274_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). No obvious morphology change and structure degradation happened except for a thickness increase by &lt;10% for all mass-loading HC anodes, showing a sustainability of the HC electrodes even at a high mass loading of 17 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/medium/am-2018-15274t_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/large/am-2018-15274t_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Electrochemical performance of the HC anodes with different mass loadings with the ether-based electrolyte: (a) galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles at a current density of 0.02 A/g, (b) gravimetric and areal specific capacities, (c) initial Coulombic efficiencies at 0.02 A/g, and (d) cycling performance at a current density of 0.2 A/g after 2 cycle activation at 0.02 A/g.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b15274/asset/images/large/am-2018-15274t_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b15274&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">A compatible electrolyte strategy is investigated to enhance the electrochemical performance of the HC anodes in NIBs toward practical applications. The results show that the better compatibility between the ether-based electrolytes and the HC anodes, such as better wettability, formation of thinner and sustainable SEI films, higher ionic conductivity, and high ion diffusion coefficient in HC, plays an important role in improving performance of HC anodes compared with the ester-based electrolytes. These account for the long cycle life and high rate performance of the HC anodes. With the enhanced kinetics, a high reversible areal capacity of 4.3 mA h/cm<sup>2</sup> was achieved, preeminent to all those reported HC anodes in the literature. Our findings shed light on the system design of NIBs from the electrolyte strategies with superior kinetics and high energy density, which make NIBs with HC anodes possible for practical applications in large-scale energy storage.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div
